<div id="review_79715075" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/79715075" />
    <a title="John Kulm" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1726988-john-kulm"><img alt="John Kulm" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592351559p2/1726988.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79715075?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Kulm" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Kulm" href="/user/show/1726988-john-kulm">John Kulm</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79715075" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16019968489276874511">I could probably learn something new each time I read this book, although, I might need a few years before I pick it up again. The book became tedious for me toward the end. I think his protege and successor Marie-Louise von Franz distills and presents Jung's ideas with more clarity. But maybe that’s just me. <br/><br/>This is a different sort of autobiography because its focus is more internal than external. In the prologue to Memories, Dreams, Reflections, Jung wrote, "In the end the only events in my l</span>
  <span id="freeText16019968489276874511" style="display:none">I could probably learn something new each time I read this book, although, I might need a few years before I pick it up again. The book became tedious for me toward the end. I think his protege and successor Marie-Louise von Franz distills and presents Jung's ideas with more clarity. But maybe that’s just me. <br /><br />This is a different sort of autobiography because its focus is more internal than external. In the prologue to Memories, Dreams, Reflections, Jung wrote, "In the end the only events in my life worth telling are those when the imperishable world interrupted into this transitory one. That is why I speak chiefly on inner experiences, amongst which I include dreams and visions.”<br /><br />I was disappointed that he wrote very little about his wife, Emma, and he said nothing about Antonia Wolff. I was curious of what he’d have to say about them, and surely these two women presented an inner experience interrupting between the imperishable world and Jung’s transitory world. Forgive my curiosity, and if you’re not curious about that sort of thing maybe you should ask yourself why you aren’t. <br /><br />Jung did go into his relationship with Freud in a very honest and revealing chapter, which I felt was a particularly interesting section.<br /><br />There’s so much to learn in this book, however, that I’m sure I’ll have to come back to it. Here’s a brief quote, for example, from a section discussing transference: “For psychotherapy to be effective a close rapport is needed, so close that the doctor cannot shut his eyes to the heights and depths of human suffering. The rapport consists, after all, in a constant comparison and mutual comprehension, in the dialectical confrontation of two opposing psychic realities. If for some reason these mutual impressions do not impinge on each other, the psychotherapeutic process remains ineffective, and no change is produced. Unless both doctor and patient become a problem to each other, no solution is found.” <br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16019968489276874511" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_694721039" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/694721039" />
    <a title="Andy McKenzie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1072037-andy-mckenzie"><img alt="Andy McKenzie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435500868p2/1072037.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/694721039?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andy McKenzie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andy McKenzie" href="/user/show/1072037-andy-mckenzie">Andy McKenzie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1072037-andy-mckenzie?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer694721039" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9342616549066366273">I went through love/hate cycles with the words, sentences, paragraphs, and themes in this book. There is much that is profound and that I found potentially useful, but there is also much that seems dangerously delusional. <br/><br/><b>Positives</b><br/><br/>1) Jung is an expert on the unconscious and that knowledge is on full display. The chapter "Confrontation with the Unconscious" is great on this. In particular, the sections near p 187 discuss how one should strive to differentiate oneself from one's unconscious conten</span>
  <span id="freeText9342616549066366273" style="display:none">I went through love/hate cycles with the words, sentences, paragraphs, and themes in this book. There is much that is profound and that I found potentially useful, but there is also much that seems dangerously delusional. <br /><br /><b>Positives</b><br /><br />1) Jung is an expert on the unconscious and that knowledge is on full display. The chapter "Confrontation with the Unconscious" is great on this. In particular, the sections near p 187 discuss how one should strive to differentiate oneself from one's unconscious contents by naming and personifying them, so as to better negotiate and vibe with them. Most mental operations are unconscious -- this much is now accepted neuropsyc wisdom -- so techniques to tap into and shape our unconscious processes deserve to percolate through our memeplexes even further. <br /><br />2) To Jung, keeping secrets involves a trade-off: the downside is that they are isolating, but the upside is that they allow and incentivize individuals keeping them to grow. This idea is fascinating and is something I have not seen explored as in-depth elsewhere. Section II of the "Last Thoughts" chapter, which focuses on this and takes it to all of its logical conclusions, is insanely money. <br /><br />3) At the end of the day, Jung was a successful clinician-scientist. As someone who aspires to straddle this divide as well, it is interesting to see how his path evolved. E.g., I empathized with his lamentation that during medical school he was "able to study Kant only on Sundays", as I too feel pressure to put more abstract pursuits on hold during M1 and M2. I also enjoyed his description of how he decided on psychiatry as a specialty once he read about "diseases of the personality" and realized that pursuing it would allow him to synthesize his various interests. Finally, I respected his decision to leave academia when he felt it was too stifling of his long-term creative autonomy. I don't want this paragraph to make it seem that I am comparing myself to him in any favorable way, since I have accomplished basically nothing, but this a major part of what I got out of the book, and an honest review demands that I acknowledge it. <br /><br /><b>Negatives</b><br /><br />1) In my view he downplays the effect of *randomness* on the events in his life. This starts in the first chapter when he interprets a childhood accident as indicating an unconscious suicidal urge (I wrote "lol what" in the margin), and continues throughout. The most salient example of this occurs in his first interaction with Freud, during which he heard a loud sound in a bookcase, interpreted it as a "catalytic exteriorization phenomenon", predicted that it would happen again, and then cheerfully reported that it did. <br /><br />I have two explanations for why he might have viewed these random, independent events as actually related to one another: a) that this was a necessary occupational hazard of his amazing ability to make connections between disparate ideas in history and psychology, and b) more charitably, he knew that any given connection between ideas might be a bit ridiculous, but wanted to encourage a culture in which discussing narratives, myths, and personal missions that might not be completely true was more acceptable. Evidence for (b) is his example about how the Taos Pueblo Native Americans had so much more meaning in their lives because they believed that their rituals were <i>literally</i> responsible for keeping the sun up in the sky. <br /><br />2) His descriptions of his clinical successes, as described throughout the book but in particular on p 143, are shockingly self-serving and non-transparent. He claims that 2/3 of his patients were at least considerably improved, and that of the 1/3 who were not improved, it was hard to tell because they may not have gotten better until many years later. Yes, but he doesn't mention that many patients may have gotten <i>worse</i> many years later and not told him; he likely doesn't mention this because of the insidious effects of confirmation bias. Data, data, data: there needed to be more of it. <br /><br />3) It seems to me that the goal of life, for him, is as much inner peace and contentment as hunter gatherers might have ideally had, rather than better living through technology. This is fine for what it is worth -- as Kevin Kelly says in *What Technology Wants*, luddites have a point -- but he does not embrace or even discuss the trade-offs that such a worldview imposes and requires. Thus I was put-off by his constant harping about how things used to be better in earlier, pre-Enlightenment eras (e.g. on p 240), and this deeply tainted the way I think about his book and life. <br /><br /><b>Quotes</b> <br /><br />On people who claim that children are innocent and cannot have interesting thoughts: <br /><br />"Oh, these good, efficient, healthy-minded people, they always remind me of those optimistic tadpoles who bask in a puddle in the sun, in the shallowest of waters, crowding together and amiably wriggling their tails, totally unaware that the next morning the puddle will have dried up and left them stranded." <br /><br />On the anxiolytic effects of nightly prayer: <br /><br />"My nightly prayer did, of course, grant me a ritual protection since it concluded the day properly and just as properly ushered in night and sleep." <br /><br />On the suffering inherent in the world (via Schopenhauer): <br /><br />"Here at last was a philosopher who had the courage to see that all was not for the best in the fundamentals of the universe." <br /><br />On what the word God meant to him: <br /><br />"This expression, "God's world," may sound sentimental to some ears. For me it did not have this character at all. To "God's world" belonged everything superhuman--dazzling light, the darkness of the abyss, the cold impassivity of infinite space and time, and the uncanny grotesqueness of the irrational world of chance. "God," for me, was everything--and anything but "edifying.""<br /><br />On the public's perception of new ideas: <br /><br />"Hitherto I had encountered only the brick wall of traditional views, but now I came up against the steel of people's prejudice and their utter incapacity to admit unconventional possibilities." <br /><br />On Freud's insensitivity: <br /><br />"When Freud visited me in Zurich in 1909, I demonstrated the case of Babette to him. Afterward he said to me, "You know, Jung, what you have found out about this patient is certainly interesting. But how in the world were you able to bear spending hours and days with this phenomenally ugly female?""<br /><br />On jealousy: <br /><br />"It frequently happens that women who do not really love their husbands are jealous and destroy their friendships. They want the husband to belong entirely to them because they themselves do not belong to him. The kernel of all jealousy is lack of love." <br /><br />Against celebrity: <br /><br />"My patients brought me so close to the reality of human life that I could not help learning essential things from them. Encounters with people of so many different kinds and on so many different psychological levels have been for me incomparably more important than fragmentary conversations with celebrities. The finest and most significant conversations of my life were anonymous." <br /><br />On objectivity: <br /><br />"The pendulum of the mind oscillates between sense and nonsense, not between right and wrong." <br /><br />On tolerance: <br /><br />"Freud himself had a neurosis, no doubt diagnosable and one with highly troublesome symptoms, as I had discovered on our voyage to America. Of course he had taught me that everybody is somewhat neurotic, and that we must practice tolerance." <br /><br />On the necessity of actually putting oneself "out there" with our words:<br /><br />"There is a tremendous difference between intending to tell something and actually telling it." <br /><br />On the trade-offs to fantasies: <br /><br />"This is the fund of unconscious images which fatally confuse the mental patient. But it is also the matrix of a mythopoeic imagination which has vanished from our rational age." <br /><br />On having something to protect: <br /> <br />"Particularly at this time, when I was working on the fantasies, I needed a point of support in "this world," and I may say that my family and my professional work were that to me. It was most essential to have a normal life in the real world as a counterpoise to that strange inner world. My family and my profession remained the base to which I could always return, assuring me that I was an actually existing, ordinary person. The unconscious contents could have driven me out of my wits. By my family, and the knowledge: I have a medical diploma from a Swiss university, I must help my patients, I have a wife and five children, I live at 228 Seestrasee in Kusnacht--these were actualities which made demands upon me and proved to me again and again that I really existed, that I was not a blank page whirling about in the winds of the spirit, like Nietzsche... For me, such irreality was the quintessence of horror, for I aimed, after all, at <i>this</i> world and <i>this</i> life. No matter how deeply absorbed or how blown about I was, I always knew that everything I was experiencing was ultimately directed at this real life of mine. I meant to meet its obligations and fulfill its meanings. My watchword was: Hic Rhodus, hic salta!["Here is Rhodes, jump here!"; meaning prove yourself here and now]."<br /><br />On the bigotry of consciousness (towards unconsciousness): <br /><br />"I was by no means free from the bigotry and hubris of consciousness which wants to believe that any halfway decent inspiration is due to one's own merit, whereas inferior reactions come merely by chance, or even derive from alien sources." <br /><br />On summarizing his life: <br /><br />"I am this bundle of what has been, and what has been accomplished." <br /><br />On the evolution of consciousness: <br /><br />"Consciousness is phylogenetically and ontogenetically a secondary phenomenon. It is time this obvious fact were grasped at last." <br /><br /><b>Random Notes</b> <br /><br />- if it seems like a patient should have a father complex, but does not seem to, Jung asks about the grandfather<br />- increasing meaning in life leads to decreased neurosis, in general, in Jung's conception <br />- he is anti-pure intellectuals, seems true and jives with the modern fusion of rationality as strictly subservient to one's emotions, goals, and values<br />- at their first meeting, Freud asked Jung to meet him at 1 pm in the afternoon, and they were up talking for 13 hours straight<br />- in Jung's telling, Freud was literally dogmatic about his sexual theory, and in Jung's interpretation, this was because Freud was worried about drifting into more mystical explanations for repressions <br />- seems like psychogenic fainting fits were much more common back in that era--is this true? If so, could it be explained by nutrition? <br />- he had one dream about a house in which deeper levels corresponded to different levels of consciousness, which was cool and reminded me of *Inception* (which was Jungian) <br />- respect Jung's intellectual honesty in breaking with Freud and being temporarily isolated from his intellectual peers as a result<br />- interesting how he started building castles with blocks as he did when he was 10 or 11 in order to deal with anxiety ("psychic disturbance") he was having, reminds me of Charlie Hoehn's idea to "play" (<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://charliehoehn.com/2013/05/19/how-i-cured-my-anxiety/">charliehoehn.com/2013/05/19/how-i-cur...</a>‎) <br />- in order to interpret dreams in Jungian style, it seems that one must posses broad knowledge of myths. How convenient, then, that this is precisely what Jung possessed <br />- symbolism throughout history that Jung found interesting: Christianity, Merlin, the Holy Grail, Goethe's *Faust*, and alchemy <br />- the science of psychology has come a long way since his work, and at least in some part that should be credited to the excitement that he stirred about it<br /><br /><b>Spaced Repetition Flashcards</b><br /><br />1) q: Biography: why couldn't Carl Jung learn math? <br /><br />a: he had moral qualms with the idea that two things which were different from one another (e.g., a and b) could be set equal<br />&gt; "whereas a = b seemed to me a downright lie or a fraud"<br /><br />2) q: Throughout history, what have boys tended to draw pictures of? <br /><br />a: battle scenes<br />&gt; and pictures of fighters, warriors <br />&gt; e.g., Carl Jung drew these as a child<br />&gt; e.g., Russian child from Middle ages, Onfirm <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.goldschp.net/SIG/onfim/onfim.html">http://www.goldschp.net/SIG/onfim/onf...</a><br /><br />3) Jung: the idea became fixed in my mind that I must live near a [...]; without [...], I thought, nobody could live at all. <br /><br />lake ... water<br />&gt; from Memories, Dreams, Reflections<br />&gt; a la Sachs, "I think I belong in the water ... I think we all belong in the water" <br /><br />4) q: What are the columns in Winer's version of Jung's association test? <br /><br />a: word, reaction time (RT), response<br /><br />&gt; for RT, leave blank if the response is basically immediate, and count the # of seconds of pause if it seems more than immediate<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.winerfoundation.org/Neurocare/Medical%20Education/Jung.html">http://www.winerfoundation.org/Neuroc...</a><br /><br />5) q: q: "What are your dreams?" <br /><br />a: "I have none". Response? (Jung) <br /><br />a: "you will soon have some" <br />&gt; "anyone else would have probably have dreamt that very night" (p 135) <br /><br />6) define collective unconscious <br /><br />ancestral patterns of memories, instincts, and experiences common to all mankind<br />&gt; these patterns are inherited, may be arranged into archetypes, and are observable through their effects on dreams, behaviour<br /><br />7) q: What was Jung's major difference from Freud w/r/t repression mechanisms during neurosis? <br /><br />a: Jung thought that they were not necessarily sexual <br />&gt; "I was familiar with numerous cases of neurosis in which the question of sexuality played a subordinate part, other factors standing in the foreground--for example, the problem of social adaptation, of oppression by tragic circumstance of life, prestige considerations, and so on" <br /><br />8) q: Accd to Jung, what was Freud's greatest accomplishment w/r/t psychiatry? <br /><br />a: taking neurotic patients seriously and entering into their peculiar individual psychology <br />&gt; "he had the courage to let the case material speak for itself ... he saw with the patient's eyes, so to speak, and so reached a deeper understanding of mental illness than had hitherto been possible" <br /><br />9) q: Accd to Jung, what was Freud's greatest accomplishment w/r/t society? <br /><br />a: evaluating dreams as a source to uncover unconscious processes <br />&gt; "the impetus which he gave to our civilization sprang from his discovery of an avenue to the unconscious... he gave back to mankind a tool that had seemed irretrievably lost" <br /><br />10) q: What was the purpose of Jung's yoga exercises? <br /><br />a: to calm himself enough to resume his work<br />&gt; with the unconscious<br />&gt; "the Indian, on the other hand, does yoga exercises in order to obliterate completely the multitude of psychic contents and images" <br /><br />11) q: What was Carl Jung's "something to protect" when he was working on understanding his fantasies? <br /><br />a: his family and his professional work<br />&gt; "it was most essential to have a normal life in the real world as a counterpoise to that strange inner world" <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://lesswrong.com/lw/nb/something_to_protect/">lesswrong.com/lw/nb/something_to_prot...</a>‎<br /><br />12) q: Accd to Jung, there is no better means of intensifying the feeling of individuality than what? <br /><br />a: the possession of a secret which the individual is pledged to guard<br />&gt; "the individual needs a secret which for various reasons he may not or cannot reveal. Such a secret reinforces him in the isolation of his individual aims. A great many individuals cannot bear this isolation... as a rule they end up by surrendering their individual goal to their craving for collective conformity--a procedure which all the opinions, beliefs, and ideals of their environment encourage." </span>
  <a data-text-id="9342616549066366273" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_437320525" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/437320525" />
    <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid"><img alt="Farhan Khalid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350661583p2/2881911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/437320525?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Farhan Khalid" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid">Farhan Khalid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=european">european</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=memoir">memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer437320525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10987464001923362041">Myth is more individual and expresses life more precisely than does science<br/><br/>These are nothing but islands of memory afloat in a sea of vagueness<br/><br/>I was deeply troubled by my mother's being away<br/><br/>I always felt mistrustful when the world "love" was spoken<br/><br/>I have trusted men friends and been disappointed<br/><br/>I have mistrusted women friends and was not disappointed<br/><br/>The prayer gave me a sense of comfort in face of the vague uncertainties<br/><br/>When I was with them I became different from the way I was at home<br/><br/>It occurre</span>
  <span id="freeText10987464001923362041" style="display:none">Myth is more individual and expresses life more precisely than does science<br /><br />These are nothing but islands of memory afloat in a sea of vagueness<br /><br />I was deeply troubled by my mother's being away<br /><br />I always felt mistrustful when the world "love" was spoken<br /><br />I have trusted men friends and been disappointed<br /><br />I have mistrusted women friends and was not disappointed<br /><br />The prayer gave me a sense of comfort in face of the vague uncertainties<br /><br />When I was with them I became different from the way I was at home<br /><br />It occurred to me that I was actually two different persons<br /><br />I had been cheated-cheated—cheated out of my beloved past<br /><br />I was living in two ages simultaneously<br /><br />Don’t think of it, just don’t think of it!<br /><br />What does God want? To act or not to act?<br /><br />Devil might be playing a trick on me<br /><br />My entire youth can be understood in terms of this secret<br /><br />It included in me an almost unendurable loneliness<br /><br />I must take the responsibility<br /><br />I remained alone with my thoughts <br /><br />Reading provided a beneficial distraction from the preoccupations of personality<br /><br />The great find resulting from my researches was Schopenhauer<br /><br />By "Will" he really meant God, the Creator, and that he was saying that God was blind<br /><br />In science I missed the factor of meaning, and in religion, that of empiricism<br /><br />Paranoid ideas and hallucinations contain a germ of meaning<br /><br />The finest and most significant conversations of my life were anonymous<br /><br />House represented a kind of image of the psyche—state of consciousness<br /><br />The ground floor stood for the first level of the unconscious<br /><br />The deeper I went, the more alien and the darker the scene became <br /><br />In the cave, I discovered remains of a primitive culture<br /><br />Dreams are a part of nature which harbors no intention to deceive, but express something as best they can<br /><br />Perhaps my conscious is performing a personality that is not me, but which is insisting on coming through to expression<br /><br />No, it is not art! On the contrary, it is nature<br /><br />I was a misunderstood artist<br /><br />Red book: esthetic elaboration of my fantasies <br /><br />Reality meant scientific comprehension<br /><br />I had base (family and profession) unlike Nietzsche<br /><br />My dream referred to alchemy, as if I was trying to solve the riddle of unknown language<br /><br />In physics, we speaks of energy and its various manifestations<br /><br />The situation in psychology is precisely the same <br /><br />Lumping all instincts under the concept of sexuality was Freud's initial error<br /><br />Psyche is transformed or developed by the relationship of ego to the contents of unconscious <br /><br />It is God who created the world and its sins, and who therefore became Christ in order to suffer the fate of humanity<br /><br />In my retiring room I am by myself<br /><br />Things which have carried me out of time into seclusion, out of the present into timelessness <br /><br />The tower became for me a place of spiritual concentration<br /><br />The small central section which crouched so low, so hidden, was myself!<br /><br />I added an upper story to this section, an extension of consciousness achieved in old age<br /><br />The tower in some way was a place of maturation—a maternal womb<br /><br />A concretization of the individuation process<br /><br />I built the house in a kind of dream <br /><br />My personality no. 2 exists outside time and the son of maternal unconscious <br /><br />I am under the influence of things or questions which were left incomplete and unanswered by my parents and grandparents and more distant ancestors<br /><br />The loss of the connection with the past has given rise to the "discontents" of civilization<br /><br />We no longer live on what we have, but on promises, in the darkness of the future<br /><br />New methods or gadgets are deceptive sweetness of existence<br /><br />Second personality see life in the round, as something forever coming into being and passing on<br /><br />Conscious, which attempted to understand and could not<br /><br />Unconscious, which wanted to express something and could not formulate it any better than a dream<br /><br />We always require an outside point to stand on, in order to apply the lever of criticism <br /><br />Everything that irritates us about others can lead us to an understanding of ourselves<br /><br />What nature leaves imperfect, the art perfects <br /><br />Man is the second creator of the world, gives it objective existence<br /><br />Africa: here country is not man's country, it's God's country<br /><br />God was in command here—in other words, not will and intention, but inscrutable design<br /><br />The moment in which light comes is God<br /><br />The moment brings redemption, release<br /><br />India affected me like a dream<br /><br />To the oriental, good and evil are meaningfully contained in nature, and are merely varying degrees of the same thing<br /><br />The Indian's goal is not perfection. He wishes to free himself from nature<br /><br />He seeks in mediation, the condition of imagelessness and emptiness <br /><br />A man who has not passed through the inferno of his passion has never overcome them<br /><br />For Buddha, the self stands above all gods<br /><br />In Christianity more is suffered, in Buddhism more is seen and done<br /><br />The anima of man has a strongly historical character<br /><br />As a personification of the unconscious she goes back into prehistory, and embodies the contents of the past<br /><br />Myths are the earlier form of science<br /><br />Myth is the natural and indispensable intermediate stage between unconscious and conscious<br /><br />My parents' marriage was not a happy one<br /><br />[In Dream] Here was my father who, after an absence of twenty-six years, wished to ask a psychologist about the newest insights and information on marital problems<br /><br />The individual who wishes to have an answer to the problem of evil has first need of self-knowledge, without self-deception and self-delusion<br /><br />We have no imagination for evil, but evil has us in its grip<br /><br />"Mana", "Daimon", and "God" are synonyms for the unconscious<br /><br />Scheme of creation: The Creator may become conscious of His creation, and man conscious of himself<br /><br />If the Creator were conscious of Himself, He would not need conscious creatures<br /><br />No science will ever replace myth<br /><br />It is not that "God" is a myth, but that myth is the revelation of a divine life in man<br /><br />The Word of God comes to us and we have no way of distinguishing whether it is different from God<br /><br />If the unconscious is anything at all, it must consist of earlier evolutionary stages of our conscious psyche<br /><br />We cannot see beyond the psyche<br /><br />We are in the deepest sense the victims and the instruments of cosmogonic "love"<br /><br />The difference between most people and myself is that for me the "dividing walls" are transparent<br /><br />Other find these walls so opaque that they see nothing behind them<br /><br />Loneliness does not come from having no people about one, but being unable to communicate the things that seems important to oneself<br /><br />A creative person has little over his own life. He is not free. He is captive and driven by daimon</span>
  <a data-text-id="10987464001923362041" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2037818957" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Hans" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Hans" href="/user/show/1142366-hans">Hans</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/165044.Memories_Dreams_Reflections">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1142366-hans?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1142366-hans?shelf=biographies">biographies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1142366-hans?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2037818957" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2869933269950886044">C.G. Jung is one of my all time favorite thinkers. What I love so much about this book was his more human moments where he showed himself to struggle with the same mundane elements of life that the rest of do. I used to have a false impression that an extremely talented individual would be equally impressive in all the other aspects of their life besides their chosen specialty. The first human moment that Jung discusses is his frustration with learning Math as a child. The way he viewed math he </span>
  <span id="freeText2869933269950886044" style="display:none">C.G. Jung is one of my all time favorite thinkers. What I love so much about this book was his more human moments where he showed himself to struggle with the same mundane elements of life that the rest of do. I used to have a false impression that an extremely talented individual would be equally impressive in all the other aspects of their life besides their chosen specialty. The first human moment that Jung discusses is his frustration with learning Math as a child. The way he viewed math he struggled with grasping the over-arching purpose behind it since it was so incredibly theoretical and disconnected from anything tangible that the rules or axioms might as well be completely arbitrary. This resonated with me since I too slogged my way through math and had similar questions in the back of my mind the entire time I was learning Algebraic Theorems. <br /><br />The book goes on to elaborate on how he arrived at his profession and developed his now well-known ideas. The part that impressed me the most was how unlike other scientists Jung didn't get lost in Empiricism and Objectivity. He still believed in the importance and value of how one experiences. I think there are pros and cons to Empiricism but to me its most dangerous is when it is so completely absorbed by Scientists that they sacrifice part of their own humanity for it and start discounting subjective experience despite that it is the primary way through which all of humanity gains an understanding of the world around them. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2869933269950886044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_530502407" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/530502407" />
    <a title="Derek Davis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1950709-derek-davis"><img alt="Derek Davis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289699546p2/1950709.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/530502407?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Derek Davis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Derek Davis" href="/user/show/1950709-derek-davis">Derek Davis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer530502407" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13058787024466680933">I can't give this book an overall rating because I so dislike Jung as he presents himself here (or it may be the way the material was assembled by his editor). Also, I've read none of his salient scientific/psychological/philosophical work – nor am I likely to after this. <br/><br/>He strikes me as an arrogant blowhard much of the time, even considering he was in his mid-80s when he allowed this personal material, for the first time, to be put in autobiographical form. I've heard that Jung had quite a sen</span>
  <span id="freeText13058787024466680933" style="display:none">I can't give this book an overall rating because I so dislike Jung as he presents himself here (or it may be the way the material was assembled by his editor). Also, I've read none of his salient scientific/psychological/philosophical work – nor am I likely to after this. <br /><br />He strikes me as an arrogant blowhard much of the time, even considering he was in his mid-80s when he allowed this personal material, for the first time, to be put in autobiographical form. I've heard that Jung had quite a sense of humor, but there's barely a trace of it here. He seems to take himself intensely seriously to the point of pomposity, and seems to take a peculiar pride in being self-contractory, which in others might prove endearing but with him comes across is annoying and grouchy.<br /><br />All that said, there were certainly points in the narrative when he brings up strikingly original outlooks on the workings of the mind and shows a broad concern for the human condition. The appendix actually paints the most human portrait of him, in the letters sent to his wife Emma during his trip to America in 1909, which have a light, airy and caring tone. <br /><br />I can't help thinking, though, that this is a last man I would want to be my analyst.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13058787024466680933" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

      greenturtleisland

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16842386-blumenfeld?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16842386-blumenfeld?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer671964820" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8415616436016638421">What a fantastic, refreshing read!<br/><br/>Jung makes a great point addressing the loss of myths in the modern society, giving insights on neuroses and psychosis, and above everything––the importance of exploring unconscious. Of course, he goes further telling what has led him to this colossal journey, meeting and parting his ways with Freud, etc. We get to know the most intimate of Jung’s core––his dreams. <br/><br/>I'd recommend this book to anyone. One could find out not only a tone about Jung and his life but </span>
  <span id="freeText8415616436016638421" style="display:none">What a fantastic, refreshing read!<br /><br />Jung makes a great point addressing the loss of myths in the modern society, giving insights on neuroses and psychosis, and above everything––the importance of exploring unconscious. Of course, he goes further telling what has led him to this colossal journey, meeting and parting his ways with Freud, etc. We get to know the most intimate of Jung’s core––his dreams. <br /><br />I'd recommend this book to anyone. One could find out not only a tone about Jung and his life but also about yourself. <br /><br />It's been overwhelming: it has helped me a lot to decipher some dreams of my own, to draw new connections that, I believe, could help me understand myself, thus to make better choices. I can't say it about many books, thus Memories, Dreams, Reflections is something very special, something to dwell on and taste its words. </span>

    <a title="Guillermo Galvan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12138532-guillermo-galvan"><img alt="Guillermo Galvan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351236549p2/12138532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/544705515?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Guillermo Galvan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Guillermo Galvan" href="/user/show/12138532-guillermo-galvan">Guillermo Galvan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer544705515" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11065900043749967619">I see Jung as a Buddha or Christ figure. Not that he’s a god, but that he provided us with a beautiful revelation about our meaning and spirituality. Along with Freud, his work formed the foundation of modern psychology. Yet, he has always been considered a radical, in his time and ours, for acknowledging the soul and its relation with the unconscious.<br/><br/>This book is unlike all his others because of his openness. It's more than just his autobiography; it is his great revelation before he died. The </span>
  <span id="freeText11065900043749967619" style="display:none">I see Jung as a Buddha or Christ figure. Not that he’s a god, but that he provided us with a beautiful revelation about our meaning and spirituality. Along with Freud, his work formed the foundation of modern psychology. Yet, he has always been considered a radical, in his time and ours, for acknowledging the soul and its relation with the unconscious.<br /><br />This book is unlike all his others because of his openness. It's more than just his autobiography; it is his great revelation before he died. The origins of all his great psychological discoveries are laid bare. Jung tells his life story with humor and charisma. His psychological development from child to adult is one of the most fascinating accounts I ever read.<br /><br />The degree of insight into his life led me to unpleasantness. His views on blacks made me shake my head at a person I greatly admire. For example, on his visit to Africa he had a paranoia of having sex with black women, or as he referred to as "going black."<br /><br />Yes, because we all know once you go black, you never... (Maybe there's some truth to it after all.)<br /><br />He was unable to get past skin color functioning as a psychological border. This otherness intensified according to pigmentation. My favorite sentence being, "<i>These were the blackest Negroes I had ever seen.</i>" There is a blunt poetry in this.<br /><br />Still, I have to give Jung his credit and say he was extremely open about race for his time. He is redeemed by statements such as, "<i>Prejudice cripples and injures the full phenomenon of psychic life.</i>"<br /><br />There's enough material on metaphysics, psychology, mythology, self-help, and religion/blasphemy to function as reference. I'd like to go into all these sections with their brilliant quotes, but paraphrasing would only lead to misinterpretation. I'm confident that <i>Memories, Dreams, Reflections</i> will be the best psychology/philosophy book I'll read this year.<br /><br />He's greatly impacted my life and I'd like to inspire others to give him a chance. I'll end this review with a quote that captures the dire importance of the mind.<br /><br />"<i>Nowadays we can see as never before that the peril which threatens all of us comes not from nature, but from man, from the psyches of the individual and the mass. The psychic aberration of man is the danger. Everything depends upon whether or not our psyche functions properly. If certain persons lose their heads nowadays, a hydrogen bomb will go off.</i>"<br /><br />(Here's a link to the PBS documentary <i>The Wisdom of the Dream</i> on youtube. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Us7XofxPX48">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Us7Xof...</a>)<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11065900043749967619" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3051953490" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3051953490" />
    <a title="Felix" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22327118-felix"><img alt="Felix" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410543537p2/22327118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3051953490?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Felix" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Felix" href="/user/show/22327118-felix">Felix</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=german">german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3051953490" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1338006890965105637"><i>In my case it must have been primarily a passionate urge toward understanding which brought about my birth. For that is the strongest element in my nature. This insatiable drive toward understanding has, as it were, created a consciousness in order to know what is and what happens, and in order to piece together mythic conceptions from the slender hints of the unknowable.</i><br/><br/>Jung has proved an even more spiritual author than I have previously imagined. In truth, I know of no other author who has ble</span>
  <span id="freeText1338006890965105637" style="display:none"><i>In my case it must have been primarily a passionate urge toward understanding which brought about my birth. For that is the strongest element in my nature. This insatiable drive toward understanding has, as it were, created a consciousness in order to know what is and what happens, and in order to piece together mythic conceptions from the slender hints of the unknowable.</i><br /><br />Jung has proved an even more spiritual author than I have previously imagined. In truth, I know of no other author who has blended science and spirituality more effortlessly. In the past, I had conceived of him more as a scientist than a mystic, but this book shows the two mixed in almost equal measure.<br /><br />I suppose the main value in this book is its comprehensive picture of a single Jungian case study. What I mean by this is that most of this book is devoted to Jung psychoanalysing himself with an (as you would expect) classically Jungian method. <i>A lot</i> of this book is dedicated to dream analysis and attempts to identify where and when a 'daemon' (or some similar being) may have affected Jung's life or decision-making. If you don't enjoy discussions of such things then you will likely find this book frustrating. Most biographical details come with this sort of transpersonal analysis attached.<br /><br />If, however, you are comfortable with this sort of thing, then this book is an absolute treasure trove. Jung, whether you like him or not, had a very brilliant mind. He is able to draw connections where they may not seem obvious and find meaning in all manner of mythologies and personal experiences. He explores these mythologies in greater depth in his discussions on his travels, although some of his description of 'the natives' could be considered somewhat dated.<br /><br /><i>The arch sin of faith, it seemed to me, was that it forestalled experience.</i><br /><br />Of course, Jung always pulls his ideas back to a broadly scientific viewpoint. He is credulous in experience, but rigorous in analysis. His every experience is accepted (no matter how easily it may have been dismissed by a less spiritual mind), but he is unrelenting in his pursuit of the reason for these experiences. Of course, this is not hard science (you could even argue that Jung's thought is not any kind of science at all), but his approach certainly aspires to the scientific.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1338006890965105637" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

</a>

    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('y/NRPcd2XrB8DNOqEHoDi86wA6uJKMafwC3dLwCsttbx1TjvfCiX2NsnZ8vnjwlUGO/kY0elSPibU5ueze9ETA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('W1kR1C/obrmeISEMldjqqpl1mmwS3fUa1djCw3b8Rsdhf3gGlLan0TkKlW1iLeB1Typ9pNxQe32OpoRyu7+0XQ==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

It is both witness and tribute to the late theory of mind—the so-called structural theory, with its stress on aggression, indeed the death drive, as the pitiless adversary of eros.Civilization and Its Discontents is one of the last of Freud\'s books, written in the decade before his death and first published in German in 1929. In it he states his views on the broad question of man\'s place in the world, a place Freud defines in terms of ceaseless conflict between the individual\'s quest for freedom and society\'s demand for conformity.\nFreud\'s theme is that what works for civilization doesn\'t necessarily work for man. Man, by nature aggressive and egotistical, seeks self-satisfaction. But culture inhibits his instinctual drives. The result is a pervasive and familiar guilt.\nOf the various English translations of Freud\'s major works to appear in his lifetime, only one was authorized by Freud himself: The Standard Edition of the Complete Psychological Works of Sigmund Freud under the general editorship of James Strachey.\nFreud approved the overall editorial plan, specific renderings of key words and phrases, and the addition of valuable notes, from bibliographical and explanatory. Many of the translations were done by Strachey himself; the rest were prepared under his supervision. The result was to place the Standard Edition in a position of unquestioned supremacy over all other existing versions.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4427252040390165799\" href=\"#\" Maslow<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.18 avg rating &mdash; 5,211 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1961\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer7906178490421645680\">\"If we wish to help humans to become more fully human, we must realize not only that they try to realize themselves, but that they are also reluctant or afraid or unable to do so. Only by fully appreciating this dialectic between sickness and health <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText7906178490421645680\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;If we wish to help humans to become more fully human, we must realize not only that they try to realize themselves, but that they are also reluctant or afraid or unable to do so. Only by fully appreciating this dialectic between sickness and health can we help to tip the balance in favor of health.&quot; Abraham MaslowAbraham Maslow\'s theories of self-actualization and the hierarchy of human needs are the cornerstone of modern humanistic psychology, and no book so well epitomizes those ideas as his classic Toward a Psychology of Being.A profound book, an exciting book, its influence continues to spread, more than a quarter century after its author\'s death, beyond psychology and throughout the humanities, social theory, and business management theory.Of course, the book\'s enduring popularity stems from the important questions it raises and the answers it provides concerning what is fundamental to human nature and psychological well-being, and what is needed to promote, maintain, and restore mental and emotional well-being. But its success also has to do with Maslow\'s unique ability to convey difficult philosophical concepts with passion, precision, and astonishing clarity, and, through the power of his words, to ignite in readers a sense of creative joy and wholeness toward which we, as beings capable of self-actualization, strive.This Third Edition makes Abraham Maslow\'s ideas accessible to a new generation of psychology students, as well as businesspeople, managers, and trainers interested in applying the study of human behavior to management techniques.An energetic and articulate scholar, Professor Maslow was the author of more than twenty books, including Eupsychian Management; Psychology of Science; Religions, Values, and Peak Experiences; Motivation and Personality; and Principles of Abnormal Psychology (with B. Mittelmann). He also edited New Knowledge in Human Values and wrote nearly one hundred articles. His teachings continue to be a staple for psychologists and psychology students.&quot;Capacities clamor to be used, and cease their clamor only when they are well used. . . . Not only is it fun to use our capacities, but it is necessary for growth. The unused skill or capacity or organ can become a disease center or else atrophy or disappear, thus diminishing the person.&quot; Abraham MaslowToward a Psychology of Being, Third EditionAbraham Maslow doesn\'t pretend to have easy answers, absolutes, or solutions that bring the relief of finalitybut he does have a deep belief in people. In this Third Edition of Toward a Psychology of Being (the original edition sold well over 100,000 copies), there is a constant optimistic thrust toward a future based on the intrinsic values of humanity. Professor Maslow states that, &quot;This inner nature, as much as we know of it so far, seems not to be intrinsically evil, but rather either neutral or positively \'good.\' What we call evil behavior appears most often to be a secondary reaction to frustration of this intrinsic nature.&quot; He demonstrates that human beings can be loving, noble, and creative, and are capable of pursuing the highest values and aspirations.This Third Edition will bring Professor Maslow\'s ideas to a whole new generation of business and psychology readers, as well as anyone interested in the study of human behavior.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"7906178490421645680\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer       var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_5752'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5752.The_Blank_Slate?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">The Blank Slate: The Modern Denial of Human Nature<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/3915.Steven_Pinker?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Steven Pinker<\/a><span title=\"Goodreads Author!\">*<\/span>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.08 avg rating &mdash; 21,377 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2002\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer11621582002001136789\">In The Blank Slate, Steven Pinker explores the idea of human nature and its moral, emotional, and political colorings. He shows how many intellectuals have denied the existence of human nature by embracing three linked dogmas: the Blank Slate (the mi<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11621582002001136789\" style=\"display:none\">In The Blank Slate, Steven Pinker explores the idea of human nature and its moral, emotional, and political colorings. He shows how many intellectuals have denied the existence of human nature by embracing three linked dogmas: the Blank Slate (the mind has no innate traits), the Noble Savage (people are born good and corrupted by society), and the Ghost in the Machine (each of us has a soul that makes choices free from biology). Each dogma carries a moral burden, so their defenders have engaged in desperate tactics to discredit the scientists who are now challenging them.\n\nPinker injects calm and rationality into these debates by showing that equality, progress, responsibility, and purpose have nothing to fear from discoveries about a rich human nature. He disarms even the most menacing threats with clear thinking, common sense, and pertinent facts from science and history.\n\nDespite its popularity among intellectuals during much of the twentieth century, he argues, the doctrine of the Blank Slate may have done more harm than good. It denies our common humanity and our individual preferences, replaces hardheaded analyses of social problems with feel-good slogans, and distorts our understanding of government, violence, parenting, and the arts.\n\nPinker shows that an acknowledgement of human nature that is grounded in science and common sense, far from being dangerous, can complement insights about the human condition made by millennia of artists and philosophers. All this is done in the style that earned his previous books many prizes and worldwide acclaim: wit, lucidity, and insight into matters great and small.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/38285.C_G_Jung">About C.G. Jung</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/38285.C_G_Jung"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1471024439p3/38285.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/38285.C_G_Jung">C.G. Jung
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3315050765781700775">Carl Gustav Jung (/jʊŋ/; German: [ˈkarl ˈɡʊstaf jʊŋ]), often referred to as C. G. Jung, was a Swiss psychiatrist and psychotherapist who founded analytical psychology. Jung proposed and developed the concepts of extraversion and introversion; archetypes, and the collective unconscious. His work has been influential in psychiatry and in the study of religion, philosophy, archeology, anthropology, l</span>
  <span id="freeText3315050765781700775" style="display:none">Carl Gustav Jung (/jʊŋ/; German: [ˈkarl ˈɡʊstaf jʊŋ]), often referred to as C. G. Jung, was a Swiss psychiatrist and psychotherapist who founded analytical psychology. Jung proposed and developed the concepts of extraversion and introversion; archetypes, and the collective unconscious. His work has been influential in psychiatry and in the study of religion, philosophy, archeology, anthropology, literature, and related fields. He was a prolific writer, many of whose works were not published until after his death.<br /><br />The central concept of analytical psychology is individuation—the psychological process of integrating the opposites, including the conscious with the unconscious, while still maintaining their relative autonomy. Jung considered individuation to be the central process of human development.<br /><br />Jung created some of the best known psychological concepts, including the archetype, the collective unconscious, the complex, and synchronicity. The Myers-Briggs Type Indicator (MBTI), a popular psychometric instrument, has been developed from Jung's theory of psychological types.<br /><br />Though he was a practising clinician and considered himself to be a scientist, much of his life's work was spent exploring tangential areas such as Eastern and Western philosophy, alchemy, astrology, and sociology, as well as literature and the arts. Jung's interest in philosophy and the occult led many to view him as a mystic, although his ambition was to be seen as a man of science. His influence on popular psychology, the "psychologization of religion", spirituality and the New Age movement has been immense.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3315050765781700775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;As a child I felt myself to be alone, and I am still, because I know things and must hint at things which others apparently know nothing of, and for the most part do not want to know.&rdquo;</span>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;The acceptance of oneself is the essence of the whole moral problem and the epitome of a whole outlook on life. That I feed the hungry, that I forgive an insult, that I love my enemy in the name of Christ -- all these are undoubtedly great virtues. What I do unto the least of my brethren, that I do unto Christ. But what if I should discover that the least among them all, the poorest of all the beggars, the most impudent of all the offenders, the very enemy himself -- that these are within me, and that I myself stand in need of the alms of my own kindness -- that I myself am the enemy who must be loved -- what then? As a rule, the Christian's attitude is then reversed; there is no longer any question of love or long-suffering; we say to the brother within us "Raca," and condemn and rage against ourselves. We hide it from the world; we refuse to admit ever having met this least among the lowly in ourselves.&rdquo;</span>
